  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1343925115" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1343925115" />
    <a title="Lee Klein " class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/90786-lee-klein"><img alt="Lee Klein " src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1594811741p2/90786.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1343925115?book_show_action=true">Jul 24, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lee Klein " class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lee Klein " href="/user/show/90786-lee-klein">Lee Klein </a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780679752608" href="/book/show/832484.Buddenbrooks">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/90786-lee-klein?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1343925115" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8888404202292253314">Bra-effin'-oh, young Mann -- I'm pretty sure this breaks the world record for precocious achievement of towering literary artistry. Published in 1899 when dude was like 25 years old. Must've taken a couple years to write. Can't imagine a current undergrad publishing something like this in a few years. But I didn't actually read Mann's text -- Mann comes to me filtered through John E. Woods's sensibility and super-steady, elastic, attentive prose style. The duo is as good as it gets. Of the four </span>
  <span id="freeText8888404202292253314" style="display:none">Bra-effin'-oh, young Mann -- I'm pretty sure this breaks the world record for precocious achievement of towering literary artistry. Published in 1899 when dude was like 25 years old. Must've taken a couple years to write. Can't imagine a current undergrad publishing something like this in a few years. But I didn't actually read Mann's text -- Mann comes to me filtered through John E. Woods's sensibility and super-steady, elastic, attentive prose style. The duo is as good as it gets. Of the four "major" Mann novels (plus the famous novella about the chicken hawk who dyes his hair, which I read 10 years ago translated by someone other than Woods or Michael Henry Heim -- we should really all write to Woods and ask him to translate the "stories of three decades"), I'd rank this ahead of neck-and-neck third and fourth place finishers <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/34440.Doctor_Faustus" title="Doctor Faustus by Thomas Mann" rel="nofollow noopener">Doctor Faustus</a> and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/88077.The_Magic_Mountain" title="The Magic Mountain by Thomas Mann" rel="nofollow noopener">The Magic Mountain</a>, but behind the monumental achievement of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/88076.Joseph_and_His_Brothers" title="Joseph and His Brothers by Thomas Mann" rel="nofollow noopener">Joseph and His Brothers</a>, the importance/impact of which deepens in my mind now that a year or so has passed since I read it. I love the thematic overlap among these novels. In "Buddenbrooks," there's the sort of transgenerational family saga he returns to thirty years later in "Joseph and His Bros," specializing in sibling conflict; Hanno and his mah's musical obsession echoes "Doctor Faustus" in advance -- the super-descriptive pages toward the very end relaying young Johann's improvisation on piano pulled out the prose stops, suggesting the confounded turbulence of youth, including in Hanno's case the suggestion of a desire to get with his cool friend Kai (I'm sure Mann's novels have launched hundreds of dissertations exploring his portraits of restrained sexuality); there's illness as in "The Magic Mountain," particularly Christian's case. But generally I was surprised how immersive this was, how quickly I entered the world of the story and lived with this middle-late 1880s generation. I expected something far more stodgy and comparatively amateur (like proto-Mann), I think, and so was pleased to move through this and find him writing at the highest level from the get-go -- it's not a "soap opera" as some on here have said, but Tony's kiss with the aspiring young doctor at the beach as a teen and the subsequent betrothal to the ridiculous obsequious Bendix Grunlich with sideburns dusted in the same gold powder they use on almonds around Xmas really got me into it -- the conflict between love and familial duty, the sort of restrictions that in the West have gone extinct for the good of society but the detriment of our literature. Loved the repeated image of the waves "raucously" crashing on the shore, their "ineluctable" succession suggestive of the passage of time, generations that emerge from the previous and give way to the next -- loved Thomas's evening of mystical insight after reading some philosophy (its Eastern overtones reminded me of Schopenhauer) and his totally realistic determination to change his life and then after a few days return to his entirely societally constrained ways. Loved the conflict between Thomas the rational conservative duty-bound for the family's sake to stand firmly in the past and Christian the globe-trotting liberated gadabout who tells a good story and cares not about convention with both feet planted in the future. Loved the conflict between the old ways and the emerging new (exemplified by the organ teacher's initial exasperated disdain for Wagner, followed by his understanding and appreciation). In the same ballpark but not quite playing the same socio-historical game as Joseph Roth's <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/54258.The_Radetzky_March__Von_Trotta_Family__1_" title="The Radetzky March (Von Trotta Family #1) by Joseph Roth" rel="nofollow noopener">The Radetzky March</a> -- unlike other Mann novels that have an eye out (often maybe semi-excessively explicitly in Magic Mt. and Dr F.) for the political situations of their time, this one seems more completely focused on its characters and their world. Loved how Mann handled Tony's every utterance with characteristic affectionate "gentle irony" -- loved how Mann never condescends to characters he knows are questionable, how he stands back and presents it with at most a suggestion of judgment. Loved the patient, thorough, consistently reinforced characterizations (Christian's roving eyes and trouble swallowing and story about Johnny Thunderstorm; Thomas's mustaches waxed and curled to extend beyond his either cheek). Loved the minor characters, particularly the early iteration of Gosch, whose first name I won't dare try to spell, awed by Gerda's beauty, with his hair combed forward over his brow. Loved how Gerda is like one of those mysterious statuesque women who glide from the shadows of ruined Gothic novel estates. Only Clara seemed undercharacterized and only once or twice did I feel a little confused about a plot point. There's so much life in here, all of it lived and "real" without a trace of post-modern or post-realist "reality hunger" techniques -- it's about as straight-up conventional pre-Joyce steady third-person narration as could be, a proper novel uber alles, the shoulders-of-giants Faulkner will stand upon when he conceives of the Compson family fallen on hard times (thanks to a review on here that mentions this parallel). Makes me want to read more 19th century lit like Dickens, Austen, Eliot. Anyway, a great novel, totally ambitious, controlled, and affective in its portrayal of a family's decline thanks mostly to the natural progression of innate individual sensibilities making their way through life -- ends on a really pessimistic note, something along the lines of "life will crush us all" -- but overall its presentation of life's deep dark richness and warmth is somehow optimistic, or at least suggests that our brief experience of however many days we're allotted is absolutely worth it. </span>
  <a data-text-id="8888404202292253314" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

<div id="review_584665308" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/584665308" />
    <a title="Dolors" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/18383778-dolors"><img alt="Dolors" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1502350013p2/18383778.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/584665308?book_show_action=true">Apr 08, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Dolors" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Dolors" href="/user/show/18383778-dolors">Dolors</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780140012149" href="/book/show/13490037-buddenbrooks">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">people in search.</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Dolors by: </span>
      Kris &amp; Kalliope, reading group
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/18383778-dolors?shelf=read-in-2013">read-in-2013</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer584665308" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10292517168989883635">This is my first Goodreads reading group experience and I have to thank both Kalliope and Kris for having pointed this work out to me and for having allowed me to participate. I also want to give thanks to all the reading partners who keep posting invaluable comments which have helped me to better grasp the nature of this novel. <br/> <br/><b>
  <i>"Life was harsh: and business, with its ruthless unsentimentality, was an epitome of life."</i>
</b>(Buddenbrooks, p.363)<br/><br/>Had I been told that an objective, even detached dep</span>
  <span id="freeText10292517168989883635" style="display:none">This is my first Goodreads reading group experience and I have to thank both Kalliope and Kris for having pointed this work out to me and for having allowed me to participate. I also want to give thanks to all the reading partners who keep posting invaluable comments which have helped me to better grasp the nature of this novel. <br /> <br /><b>
  <i>"Life was harsh: and business, with its ruthless unsentimentality, was an epitome of life."</i>
</b>(Buddenbrooks, p.363)<br /><br />Had I been told that an objective, even detached depiction of the downfall of a merchant family in a North-German town in the nineteenth century would shake me they way <i>Buddenbrooks</i> has shaken me, I wouldn't have believed it possible. But what I find most impacting is that even though I was prepared to witness the much forewarned decline of this family I was swept away completely all the same by the pragmatic but intense tone of the narrative which stirred unintended, troubled feelings in me.<br />Told in an omniscient, impartial voice and taking for background the first symptoms of major social and economic changes in Germany on its way into 20th Century modernity and uncertainty, Mann opens the narration with an opulent banquet in 1835 where the three generation of Buddendbrooks are celebrating their social and economic prominence and future prospects. Mann describes their world in detail and masterly pictures the characters with all their hopes, fears and ambitions, all this in a brilliantly flowing language.<br />The story mainly follows two of the children: Thomas, the crown prince who has been prepared to take over the firm and to become the future ruling man in the family, and his beautiful sister Antoine, a spoiled, naive creature with bourgeois airs but good-natured heart who will see her life expectations vanish and her dreams disappear as years go by.<br />While Thomas embodies the vitality, strength and vigor of a prosperous, responsible merchant of the time, his hypochondriac, indolent brother Christian and eventually Thomas’ introverted and frail son, Hanno, fail their merchant inheritance in allowing their artistic vocation to prevail over their duty to the firm, condemning the Buddenbrook name into oblivion.<br />In this sense, Mann sets the tone for some themes in his forthcoming works, one of them being the refined and sophisticated artistic attitude opposed to the simple, healthy and pragmatic life of a merchant family, a poignant subject in this novel and one which could also have reminiscences of his own personal experience.<br /><br />Although Mann treats his characters lovingly he always keeps in an <i>ironic distance</i> which reminds the reader that <i>the fate of the Buddenbrooks is a sealed one</i> and that, like in life, eventual decay and ultimate death can’t be prevented. And this <b> natural cycle of ups and downs </b> both of the firm and the family, for they are bound together, is precisely what makes possible that a naturalistic story such as this one could reach one’s soul and fill it with wonder with its delicate and effortless language.<br /><br />In the end, nothing is left, no grand house, no flourishing firm, no prominent family. The Buddenbrooks sink back into meaninglessness. Only an old volume with the genealogy of the whole family remains, <b>echo of a long gone world</b> and the only proof of what once was and never will be again. <br /><i>But with the end comes freedom.</i><br /><br /><b>
  <i>"Was not every human being a mistake and a blunder? Was he not in painful arrest from the hour of his birth? Prison, prison, bonds and limitations everywhere! The human being stares hopelessly through the barred window of his personality at the high walls of outward circumstances, till Death and calls him home to freedom!"</i>
</b> (p.506)<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="10292517168989883635" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_614182660" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/614182660" />
    <a title="Mala" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5960716-mala"><img alt="Mala" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1435586901p2/5960716.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/614182660?book_show_action=true">May 13, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mala" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mala" href="/user/show/5960716-mala">Mala</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5960716-mala?shelf=classic-ever-enduring-appeal">classic-ever-enduring-appeal</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5960716-mala?shelf=nobel-prize-winner">nobel-prize-winner</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer614182660" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17247907615624045068"><br/><br/>Decline of a great family always evokes interest– people watch it like they would–a train wreck, a road-side accident, or a disaster movie– with fascination.<br/>There's also an element of schadenfreude involved in "How the mighty have fallen!"<br/><br/>In this group read of Mann's first novel <i>Buddenbrooks: The Decline of a Family</i>, we got to watch up close and personal, the Buddenbrooks- their joys & sorrows, triumphs & defeats, honour & cowardice, births, marriages, divorces & deaths, rise & fall– in short, a</span>
  <span id="freeText17247907615624045068" style="display:none"><br /><br />Decline of a great family always evokes interest– people watch it like they would–a train wreck, a road-side accident, or a disaster movie– with fascination.<br />There's also an element of schadenfreude involved in "How the mighty have fallen!"<br /><br />In this group read of Mann's first novel <i>Buddenbrooks: The Decline of a Family</i>, we got to watch up close and personal, the Buddenbrooks- their joys &amp; sorrows, triumphs &amp; defeats, honour &amp; cowardice, births, marriages, divorces &amp; deaths, rise &amp; fall– in short, a very intimate portrait of a prosperous North German business family, in the backdrop of social &amp; political changes during the turbulent times of 1848 Revolutions, the Austro-Prussian War, &amp; finally the unification of Germany.<br /><br />But the politics remains peripheral to the story and the Buddenbrooks never really become symbolic of a bygone time/ way of life cause there are other old familes that are surviving/ flourishing along with the rise of nouveau riche like the Hagenströms– their fall seems to result more from peculiarities of temperament &amp; unfortunate circumstances than anything else. The historical change is sometimes seen as desirable as in the musical progression from Bach to Beethoven to Wagner. It's only towards the end, that Mann categorically regrets the changing times in the description of Hanno's school:<br /><br /><i> Soon after the new director's arrival, renovation and modernization of the old institution had begun– according to the very latest hygienic and aesthetic criteria– and been very successfully completed. It remained an open question, however, whether the school had not been a more sympathetic and generous institution in the old days–when a little less modern, comfort and a little more kindness, sentiment, serenity, benevolence, and good cheer had held sway in its rooms. </i> (708)<br /><br />Usually in a narrative of this sort, there's some family sin committed in the past, the consequences of which are then visited upon the descendants- that's how it plays out in popular narratives like Conan Doyle's <i>The Hound of Baskervilles</i> to Poe's <i>The Fall of the House of Usher</i> and in Hawthorne's <i>The House of the Seven Gables</i>, but no such thing here.<br /><br />Mann was writing in the 19th century realistic tradition of Theodor Fontane &amp; Anton Chekhov. There is also the hovering pessimism of Schopenhauer.<br />Now Mann is an intellectual writer &amp; that works fine when he is showing his astute grasp of human psychology &amp; his subtle display of irony but not when it comes to the subject matter– the decline of a family– a highly emotional topic, which is, however, given an almost cool, ironical treatment– so I ended up registering it as a 'fact' but not as an 'emotion'- &amp; therein lies the difference &amp; the missing fifth ★<br /><br /><b>My biggest grouse is against the treatment of its purported theme of commercial life versus an artistic one– </b><br /><br />Now there are only two bonafide artistic characters here– Thomas B's wife Gerda &amp; their son Hanno (&amp; two minor ones–a church organist-cum-piano teacher, &amp; a military guy, a fellow musician who connects with Gerda). The younger Buddenbrook, Christian the hypochondriac's tragedy is having an artistic temperament but not the ability. He could only be a second-rate standup comic &amp; a vagabond. In a way, his siblings Thomas &amp; Tony B's lives are shown as 'performance' too–the former does it out of "vanity," the latter out of a sense of "family dignity."<br /><br />In family sagas, it's expected of each member that, in their individual way, they would all contribute to the family name &amp; continue its legacy but here the whole burden falls on Thomas Buddenbrook's able &amp; elegant shoulders– unlike his passive/dissolute/weak siblings, Thomas is the only one who is action-oriented, quintessential German male- precise, organized, tradition-bound, cold &amp; hard– but one swallow doesn't make a summer &amp; he suffers mid-life crisis &amp; burn out :<br /><i>His interior life was impoverished, had undergone a deterioration so severe that it was like the almost constant burden of some vague grief. And bound up with it all was an implacable sense of personal duty and the grim determination to present himself at his best, to conceal his frailties by any means possible, and to keep up appearances. It had all contributed to making his existence what it was: artificial, self-conscious, and forced – until every word, every gesture, the slightest deed in the presence of others had become a taxing and grueling part in a play.</i><br /><br />But it's the way Hanno's life* arc is presented that's unbearable– born of a passionless union, the last of the Buddenbrooks, a chronically sick child, he is embedded with a death wish. Now I'm sick &amp; tired of the constant depiction in fiction, of creative people– musicians, writers, artists– as wishy-washy, namby-pamby, cry babies!<br /><br />Artistic life, with its no guarantee of success, no fixed income, fluctuating fortunes, demands nerves of steel. Granted, creative types are highly sensitive, need kindness &amp; support, but then who doesn't? Why single them out as freaks? <b>Strange, cause Mann is writing a realistic novel yet his concept of the artist is a highly romanticised* one! </b> Proust's narrator in <i>Swann's Way</i>, doesn't cry as much as Hanno does! Even when happiness is a possibility <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ Thomas Buddenbrook, in a supreme act of understanding &amp; sacrifice, relieves his son of the burden of carrying the family business forward.  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>, the narrative moves inexorably towards tragedy. In its fixation with 'decline,' the plot becomes predictable.<br /><br /><b>Show or Tell? </b><br />Tell me a story, by all means, still sometimes it's quite a task to align modern sensibilties with the reading of 18th-19th century style of fictioning, for example, the descriptive prose here does serve a purpose ( establishibg realistic historical context in terms of fashion, life style, characters' personalities etc), but at times, it was like finding delicious ironies in Dickens' narrative &amp; then just a few pages later, having it hammered home that what you just read, was indeed irony!<br />Now I have problem even reading those "He said" &amp; "She said" manner of narration what then to speak of descriptive prose but to each their own. Curiously enough though, as the year changed ( the novel spans a period of 1835-77), the narrative style also changed– there were pages with only dialogues without naming their speakers ( what a relief!), the epistolary chapters were fun too.<br /><br /><i> Incisive characterizations are achieved through a witty use of German dialects and the adaptation of leitmotif techniques borrowed from Wagner. And the fast-paced narrative is tightly controlled by a structure evident in the parallel between the first chapter and the last: both take place on rainy evenings in the fall, and both feature Tony Buddenbrook in conversations about religion -- first with her rationally skeptical grandfather and at the end with her aged teacher, who has always waged the good fight "against the onslaughts of reason."**</i><br /><br />Comparisons are odious in real life but in literature they serve a valuable function : <br /><br />I came to the Buddenbrooks read from another group read of a multi-generational saga– G G Márquez's <i>One Hundred Years of Solitude</i>– &amp; I can't help but wonder, how magical realism freed Márquez to add multiple rich layers to his tale &amp; expand his fictional universe whereas Mann is somewhat constrained by realism– sometimes reality is just not enough!<br />Still, as a twenty-five year old, writing his first novel, this is a bravura performance! It won him the Nobel prize for literature, let's not forget that! This is Mann, so there's philosophy &amp; there's music– &amp; they are the finest written portions in this book. With the fall of grand old families, a way of life, values &amp; culture are also obliterated &amp; sometimes the replacements are not really that good or desirable.<br /><br />(*) Mann apparently based Hanno's character on Goethe's Young Werther.<br /><br />(**) <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.nytimes.com/books/97/09/21/reviews/mann-buddenbrooks.html">http://www.nytimes.com/books/97/09/21...</a><br /><br />* * *<br /><i> What sort of people prefer the monotony of the sea...it's those who have gazed too long and too deeply into the complexity at the heart of things and so have no choice but to demand one thing from external realitiy: simplicity.</i><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="17247907615624045068" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/622607033" />

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/622607033?book_show_action=true">May 23, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lawyer" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lawyer" href="/user/show/3025547-lawyer">Lawyer</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Anyone</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Lawyer by: </span>
      Kris Rabberman
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3025547-lawyer?shelf=german-literature">german-literature</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3025547-lawyer?shelf=thomas-mann">thomas-mann</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3025547-lawyer?shelf=20th-century">20th-century</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3025547-lawyer?shelf=kindred-spirits-and-others">kindred-spirits-and-others</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3025547-lawyer?shelf=nobel-prize-winner">nobel-prize-winner</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3025547-lawyer?shelf=2013">2013</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3025547-lawyer?shelf=group-read">group-read</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer622607033" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15612131087906770762">I am especially grateful for Kris Rabberman's invitation to join this group read. My experience with <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/19405.Thomas_Mann' title='Thomas Mann' rel='nofollow noopener'>Thomas Mann</a> had been limited to the short novella <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/53061.Death_in_Venice' title='Death in Venice by Thomas Mann' rel='nofollow noopener'>Death in Venice</a>. This group has broadened my reading horizons. Without the enthusiasm of the moderators and group members, it is highly unlikely I would have turned toward <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/80890.Buddenbrooks_The_Decline_of_a_Family' title='Buddenbrooks The Decline of a Family by Thomas Mann' rel='nofollow noopener'>Buddenbrooks: The Decline of a Family</a>.<br/><br/>My review will be forthcoming, with the added proviso that I am woefully behind on my reviewing. However, in short, <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/80890.Buddenbrooks_The_Decline_of_a_Family' title='Buddenbrooks The Decline of a Family by Thomas Mann' rel='nofollow noopener'>Buddenbrooks: The Decli</a></span>
  <span id="freeText15612131087906770762" style="display:none">I am especially grateful for Kris Rabberman's invitation to join this group read. My experience with <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/19405.Thomas_Mann" title="Thomas Mann" rel="nofollow noopener">Thomas Mann</a> had been limited to the short novella <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/53061.Death_in_Venice" title="Death in Venice by Thomas Mann" rel="nofollow noopener">Death in Venice</a>. This group has broadened my reading horizons. Without the enthusiasm of the moderators and group members, it is highly unlikely I would have turned toward <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/80890.Buddenbrooks_The_Decline_of_a_Family" title="Buddenbrooks The Decline of a Family by Thomas Mann" rel="nofollow noopener">Buddenbrooks: The Decline of a Family</a>.<br /><br />My review will be forthcoming, with the added proviso that I am woefully behind on my reviewing. However, in short, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/80890.Buddenbrooks_The_Decline_of_a_Family" title="Buddenbrooks The Decline of a Family by Thomas Mann" rel="nofollow noopener">Buddenbrooks: The Decline of a Family</a> is the story of generations of the Buddenbrook family. They are merchants in the grain industry. Mann covers the generations from the time of family members who lived through the Napoleonic Wars through the end of a dynasty. It is that "decline" of a family that provided this reader with constant fascination.<br /><br />The history of a disunited Germany floats through the history of the Buddenbrooks, always in the shadows. Events have little apparent effect on the family. I strongly recommend doing a bit of supplementary reading regarding the Revolution of 1848-1849, The German- Austrian War, The Franco-Prussian War, and Otto von Bismark. Mann's references to these events are quite subtle, which serves as a background to which the Buddenbrooks seem to turn a blind eye.<br /><br />Turning once again to the dynamics of this group read, the Moderators, Kris and Kalliope have done a masterful job. Discussion among those reading the novel has been lively and thoughtful. If you're looking for a way to wrap your mind around a book which has given you pause in the past, I can assure you there's a group of "Kindred Spirits" out there, each with their particular strengths involving cultural and historical influences. Highly recommended band of readers.</span>

It is astonishing that he could write so brilliantly at the age of twenty-five. His observations have already that same sharp wit as erupting in The Magic Mountain. However splendid the novel is, I will probably never re</span>
  <span id="freeText9469625984012010631" style="display:none">I will refrain from posting a review of Buddenbrooks as I have nothing to add to the many splendid reviews of the novel. See for example Kalliope’s, Fionnuala’s or Roger’s. <br /><br />There can be no doubt that Thomas Mann must be residing on the most elevated of clouds in writers’ heaven. It is astonishing that he could write so brilliantly at the age of twenty-five. His observations have already that same sharp wit as erupting in The Magic Mountain. However splendid the novel is, I will probably never re-read Buddenbrooks as the feelings of decline and doom really get to you and are never elevated for just a moment, only now and then with a sparkle of music or a stay at the sea. In that respect, Buddenbrooks harbours a much more pessimistic atmosphere than The Magic Mountain which I will surely re-read again some day.</span>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6354112-perry?shelf=libri-classici">libri-classici</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer583229206" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9386439559340619137"><b>
  <i>"That all those charms have pass'd away, <br/>I might have watch'd through long decay...."</i>
</b><br/>"And Thou art Dead, as Young and Fair," Lord Byron<br/><br/>Thomas Mann's moving 1901 saga of the Buddenbrooks, a respected, wealthy family of grain merchants, begins in 1835 at the death of the patriarch. The three successive generations suffer a decline in their finances and family ideals as values change and old hierarchies are upset by Germany's rapid industrialization. Two of the siblings, Thomas and Antonie, sub</span>
  <span id="freeText9386439559340619137" style="display:none"><b>
  <i>"That all those charms have pass'd away, <br />I might have watch'd through long decay...."</i>
</b><br />"And Thou art Dead, as Young and Fair," Lord Byron<br /><br />Thomas Mann's moving 1901 saga of the Buddenbrooks, a respected, wealthy family of grain merchants, begins in 1835 at the death of the patriarch. The three successive generations suffer a decline in their finances and family ideals as values change and old hierarchies are upset by Germany's rapid industrialization. Two of the siblings, Thomas and Antonie, subordinate their personal happiness to the welfare of the family business. Antonie in particular gives up happiness <i>twice</i> for appearance's sake, each time being ravaged by reverses.<br /><br />While Mann wrote this novel largely in an objective manner, the story represents a condemnation of the decadence of a materialistic society, as shown through this family. While the Buddenbrooks were naturally honest and good, imbued with love of family, they were also afflicted by a blind loyalty to their own class. They viewed each significant event in their lives, such as births, deaths, marriages, and social decisions, in relation to its effects on the family business. Their refusal to adapt to changing conditions, to act from their moral convictions rather than treating their business as a religion, and to accept those not of their class led to their destruction. <br /><br />Mann showed an incredible attention to the descriptive details of the period as well as his affinity for leitmotifs such as those derived from his love of the operas of Richard Wagner. For example, blue skin and yellow teeth to represent decay and decadence in the family members.<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="9386439559340619137" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_814549634" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/814549634" />
    <a title="Roy Lotz" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/14046996-roy-lotz"><img alt="Roy Lotz" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1443324405p2/14046996.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/814549634?book_show_action=true">May 20, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Roy Lotz" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Roy Lotz" href="/user/show/14046996-roy-lotz">Roy Lotz</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/14046996-roy-lotz?shelf=novels-novellas-short-stories">novels-novellas-short-stories</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/14046996-roy-lotz?shelf=germanophilia">germanophilia</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer814549634" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13419871845066697851">This novel is a crowd-pleaser. Without difficult prose or avant-garde innovation, Mann has delivered a work of enduring art, a satisfying social novel of Germany in the 1800s. Indeed, the novel is so easily digestible that I find that I have very little to say about it. But lacking ideas is no excuse for not writing.<br/><br/>As with much of Mann’s work, this novel—his first major production, finished at the tender age of 26—is dominated by a gently ironic tone. Mann’s irony is not as pervasive or as deep</span>
  <span id="freeText13419871845066697851" style="display:none">This novel is a crowd-pleaser. Without difficult prose or avant-garde innovation, Mann has delivered a work of enduring art, a satisfying social novel of Germany in the 1800s. Indeed, the novel is so easily digestible that I find that I have very little to say about it. But lacking ideas is no excuse for not writing.<br /><br />As with much of Mann’s work, this novel—his first major production, finished at the tender age of 26—is dominated by a gently ironic tone. Mann’s irony is not as pervasive or as deep as Cervantes’s, wherein everything dissolves into its own contradiction. Rather, Mann’s irony constitutes a sort of refined intellectual distance, allowing Mann to be both critical and affectionate towards his bourgeois German characters (who are, after all, stand-ins for Mann’s own family). What emerges is, I think, a fair picture of this bygone world: well-meaning, but privileged people, muddling through history and misfortune.<br /><br />Although Mann’s novel is broadly similar to many a Victorian novel in its technique and content, some differences stand out for mention. For one, unlike so many classic novels, the plot of <i>Buddenbrooks</i> is not pushed forward by love and marriage. Instead, the motivations of the major characters are refreshingly diverse: money, respect, art, power, filial tenderness, tradition, and broader social currents.<br /><br />Of course, this being a realistic work, there is love and marriage. Unlike most previous novels, however, the action of this book takes place at a time when divorce is possible—which quite dramatically changes things (although it does not much improve the lot of the female characters in this book). The plot of so many 18th century novels—<i>Anna Karenina</i>, <i>Madame Bovary</i>, <i>The Red and the Black</i>—would simply be incoherent if divorce had been easily attainable, so it was quite interesting for me to see how a major novelist adapted his plot to this new possibility.<br /><br />While I would rank this novel significantly lower than <i>The Magic Mountain</i> in artistic vision and emotional depth, for simple reading pleasure <i>Buddenbrooks</i> is difficult to surpass. And, often, that is all we really need.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13419871845066697851" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_609967900" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/609967900" />
    <a title="Sue" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3642045-sue"><img alt="Sue" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1274829293p2/3642045.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/609967900?book_show_action=true">May 07, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sue" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sue" href="/user/show/3642045-sue">Sue</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3642045-sue?shelf=read-2013">read-2013</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3642045-sue?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3642045-sue?shelf=library-book">library-book</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3642045-sue?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3642045-sue?shelf=germany">germany</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer609967900" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9379057648901482169">After thinking about this book for a bit I've decided it's now or never for this review. I've also decided to increase my rating to 5 after contemplating my primary reason for marking it a 4...the tedious description of Hanno Buddenbrook's day at school. Discussion with others has caused me to look at this somewhat differently, as a portrayal of an unbearable situation during what was proving to be a quite unhappy life. All of this fits with Mann's subtitle "The Decline of a Family".<br/><br/>The novel pr</span>
  <span id="freeText9379057648901482169" style="display:none">After thinking about this book for a bit I've decided it's now or never for this review. I've also decided to increase my rating to 5 after contemplating my primary reason for marking it a 4...the tedious description of Hanno Buddenbrook's day at school. Discussion with others has caused me to look at this somewhat differently, as a portrayal of an unbearable situation during what was proving to be a quite unhappy life. All of this fits with Mann's subtitle "The Decline of a Family".<br /><br />The novel presents the history of the Buddenbrook family over several generations in their home city of Lúbeck. They have lived as grain merchants, certain of their own place in society and the world (and the next world too). All members are raised to live for the family's betterment. But there are cracks even in the earliest generation we meet, where the patriarch cannot love the firstborn son whose existence meant the death of his first wife. As his second son was to say:<br /><br /><br /> "A family has to be united, to hold together, Father;<br /> otherwise evil will come knocking at the door."<br /><br /><br />This need for unity might come back to haunt Johann and the family. <br /><br />Johann's generation maintains the illusion of success through carefully arranged marriages and alliances, remaining in Lúbeck while much is changing in other parts of Germany and the world. Success is often on paper, while happiness is not considered a necessary ingredient of life. <br /><br />And then the next generation, the generation of Johann's children, Tom, Christian and daughter Tony. Each has his own burden to carry for the sake of the family firm. Tony is chattel of a sort to bring income. Tom is to carry on the name. Christian...well he does not fit any role as things devolve.<br /><br />Tom is the identity of the family in Lübeck. He is Buddenbrooks. But who is he.<br /><br /><br /> "No doubt about it---Thomas Buddenbrook's existence was<br /> no different from that of an actor, but one whose whole<br /> life has become a single production, down to the smallest,<br /> most workaday detail---a production that, apart from a few<br /> brief hours each day, constantly engaged and devoured all<br /> his energies. He completely lacked any ardent interest<br /> that might have occupied his mind. His interior life was<br /> impoverished, had undergone a deterioration so severe that<br /> it was like the almost constant burden of some vague grief.<br /> And bound up with it all was an implacable sense of<br /> personal duty and the grim determination to present himself<br /> at his best, to conceal his frailties by any means possible, <br /> and to keep up appearances."<br /><br /><br />Neither work, nor marriage, nor parenthood provided any ease for Tom. <br /><br />Ah the Buddenbrook legacy, like one of those creatures of the wild, devouring its young.<br /><br />I do recommend this book heartily. Though it is lengthy, Mann provides many shifts in time, setting and characters as the reader moves though the generations. There are comic moments to counter the sadness and irony scattered throughout.<br /><br />Thanks to all the members of the Buddenbrooks Group for opening up so many levels of this book to me (most of which are not revealed in this review!).</span>
  <a data-text-id="9379057648901482169" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_103368915" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

    <a title="Jonfaith" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2327886-jonfaith"><img alt="Jonfaith" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1534772484p2/2327886.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/103368915?book_show_action=true">May 18, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jonfaith" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jonfaith" href="/user/show/2327886-jonfaith">Jonfaith</a>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

<span id="freeTextContainer17984011359761564319">Apparently this was Faulkner's favorite novel from Mann. Aspects of it likely permeated his epic of the Compson family. Coincidentally I read this one while my wife's sister was staying with us over the holidays. The Sound and The Fury was read in 2004 when we visted her in London.<br/><br/>I thought of this novel yesterday while reading Nancy Mitford's Pursuit of Love. One almost needs to polish silver when pondering these works.</span>

<div id="review_607037421" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/607037421" />
    <a title="Paul" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4643325-paul"><img alt="Paul" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1535048395p2/4643325.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/607037421?book_show_action=true">May 04, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Paul" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Paul" href="/user/show/4643325-paul">Paul</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4643325-paul?shelf=european-novels">european-novels</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer607037421" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16554903692862939867">Mann's first novel and quite a saga it is; a family history stretching through the latter half of the nineteenth century and over three/four generations. It is more accessible than some of Mann's later works as here all the big topics that Mann raises are couched in routine and the daily rhythm of life. In his first novel Mann is writing about what he knows; these are the people and details of his childhood and upbringing, clearly set in his hometown of Lubeck.<br/>I was a little reminded of the Pall</span>
  <span id="freeText16554903692862939867" style="display:none">Mann's first novel and quite a saga it is; a family history stretching through the latter half of the nineteenth century and over three/four generations. It is more accessible than some of Mann's later works as here all the big topics that Mann raises are couched in routine and the daily rhythm of life. In his first novel Mann is writing about what he knows; these are the people and details of his childhood and upbringing, clearly set in his hometown of Lubeck.<br />I was a little reminded of the Palliser novels, but though there are similarities, Trollope was more of a plain storyteller. There is always much more going on with Mann. I can imagine scholars of literature writing articles like "Dentistry and Death in Buddenbrroks". Decay and decadence are important themes. The tensions that the desire for an artistic life creates in a conventional bourgeois household. Yet there is an element of soap opera too; Thomas Buddenbrook upstairs reading Schopenauer whilst his wife is downstairs having a musical dalliance with a lieutenant. The typhoid motif appears for the first, but not the last time; rearing its head again in "Death in Venice".<br />Of all the characters; for me the most significant is Antonie (Tony), who is in the book from beginning to end. Tony is the custodian, resilient; surviving life's setbacks. Her life is unfulfilled and she never forgets her youthful attempt at rebellion; this is clear at the end of the book. She also attempts to keep the peace between her two brothers.<br />It is an impressive novel which straddles two centuries in its creation. Anthony Heilbut argues that Mann here creates a transition from Wagner and Nietzsche to Freud and Schonberg. There is always a sense of the fragility of life; death is always close and often the end is sudden. The book also ends quite suddenly. There remains an interesting undercurrent; Hanno is quite a novel character. He is uniterested in games, a target for bullies, an aesthete. Kai and Hanno provide a homoerotic undercurrent as Mann explores what is to be a recurrent theme in his novels. An interesting precursor to those later novels.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16554903692862939867" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_623530064" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/623530064" />
    <a title="Rod" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3973093-rod"><img alt="Rod" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1592245072p2/3973093.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/623530064?book_show_action=true">May 24, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Rod" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Rod" href="/user/show/3973093-rod">Rod</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3973093-rod?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3973093-rod?shelf=everymans-library">everymans-library</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3973093-rod?shelf=in-translation">in-translation</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3973093-rod?shelf=mann-oh-mann">mann-oh-mann</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3973093-rod?shelf=owned">owned</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3973093-rod?shelf=nobel-prize-winners">nobel-prize-winners</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer623530064" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4335590213945656419">In the introduction there is an anecdote about a female friend of Mann's who read <i>Buddenbrooks</i> and liked it very much, saying she was never bored by it, but she was unable to explain <i>why</i>. Word.<br/><br/>I've never liked books like this; you know, multi-generational family sagas, sort of soap opera-ish. Perhaps it's because I was bored to tears as a kid by TV miniseries like <i>East of Eden</i> and <i>Rich Man, Poor Man</i>. After reading <i>Buddenbrooks</i>, though, I realize that maybe I just <i>thought</i> I've never liked books l</span>
  <span id="freeText4335590213945656419" style="display:none">In the introduction there is an anecdote about a female friend of Mann's who read <i>Buddenbrooks</i> and liked it very much, saying she was never bored by it, but she was unable to explain <i>why</i>. Word.<br /><br />I've never liked books like this; you know, multi-generational family sagas, sort of soap opera-ish. Perhaps it's because I was bored to tears as a kid by TV miniseries like <i>East of Eden</i> and <i>Rich Man, Poor Man</i>. After reading <i>Buddenbrooks</i>, though, I realize that maybe I just <i>thought</i> I've never liked books like this, when actually I really dig them. In any case, I really dug <i>this</i> one. I could just be attracted to Mann's wondrous prose; his children called him "Die Zauberer" for a reason, I imagine. Regardless, I was drawn to the Buddenbrooks in <i>Buddenbrooks</i>. They're real. I <i>know</i> these people. Even though I've never been and probably never will be part of the aristocracy, I nevertheless found a lot to identify with in these characters. I didn't completely identify with Thomas or Christian, but I did see aspects of myself in both. The same goes for little Hanno, probably more so than I would care to admit. And Tony. Tony, Tony, Tony. Without naming names (I do have family members on Goodreads), do I ever know Tony. I'm sure I'm not alone; undoubtedly anyone with family is going to feel a certain sting of recognition at some point during the reading of <i>Buddenbrooks</i>. This just tells me that Mann is writing something that is real and applicable to life. <br /><br />This is the second time I've been under The Magician's spell, the first being during my reading of <i>The Magic Mountain</i>, and I'm sure it won't be the last, as I have some more Mann lined up to read. And perhaps there should be some more multi-generational family sagas in my future. What's that? I think I hear the Forsytes calling me.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="4335590213945656419" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_608361615" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/608361615" />
    <a title="Kim" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4542979-kim"><img alt="Kim" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1454402751p2/4542979.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/608361615?book_show_action=true">May 06, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kim" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kim" href="/user/show/4542979-kim">Kim</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer608361615" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6272087502974454392"><br/>This is a novel I may never have read had my friend Kalliope not invited me to join in a group read. While I'm an enthusiatic reader of 19th century English and (to a lesser extent) French literature, my exposure to German literature of this period has been sadly lacking. So I'm glad to have had the opportunity to read this novel along with other neophytes and with experts in German literature. While I mostly lurked on its fringes, the group discussion has been informative and stimulating.<br/><br/>To a l</span>
  <span id="freeText6272087502974454392" style="display:none"><br />This is a novel I may never have read had my friend Kalliope not invited me to join in a group read. While I'm an enthusiatic reader of 19th century English and (to a lesser extent) French literature, my exposure to German literature of this period has been sadly lacking. So I'm glad to have had the opportunity to read this novel along with other neophytes and with experts in German literature. While I mostly lurked on its fringes, the group discussion has been informative and stimulating.<br /><br />To a large extent, the plot of this novel is disclosed in the title and all the rest is detail. It concerns a prosperous and well-respected family from the mercantile middle class of northern Germany, whose wealth and social status are dissipated with each passing generation until the family effectively disappears altogether. Mann concentrates on two characters in particular, Thomas Buddenbrook and his sister Antonie. Both identify strongly with their family and make decisions based on what they believe is owed to its history, its reputation and its continued prosperity. Neither is destined for happiness. <br /><br />Mann's prose and descriptive ability are superb. Or at least I assume they are, because they've been rendered beautifully in the translation by John E Woods. The story is told through an omnipresent, omniscient, rather detached and ironic narrator. This is both a strength and weakness of the work. While the narrator is able to explore the perspective and motivation of the various characters, the detachment and irony of the narrator's voice may be the reason I didn't feel fully engaged with the characters. I would have liked to have been more moved by the characters than I was. For me, the fate of the Buddenbrook family was of more intellectual than emotional interest. <br /><br />Oscar Wilde famously defined a cynic as someone who knows the price of everything and the value of nothing. The Buddenbrooks certainly knew the price of everything. I don't know that they were necessarily cynical, but most members of the family had a warped idea of the value of their family, seeing it only in terms of what it owned and what it could buy. Mann's account of the family's downfall is a salutary lesson of the dangers of being the kind of person described by Oscar Wilde. <br /><br />I've now moved on to another 19th century middle class family saga, <i>The Man of Property</i>, the first novel in John Galsworthy's <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/103159.The_Forsyte_Saga" title="The Forsyte Saga by John Galsworthy" rel="nofollow noopener">The Forsyte Saga</a>. It will be interesting to see Galsworthy's take on middle class sensibilities in a changing world. </span>
  <a data-text-id="6272087502974454392" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer3427214902" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer2835345344049339588">Accurate to his elegant style, detailed and generous in the petty small details, Thomas Mann returned with this voluminous novel in which narrates the decline and anguish of a German family of the late nineteenth century.<br/>All the complexes that can live (and suffer) people of the upper class, when they see themselves in distressing needs, are described here, with a halo of nostalgia that surrounds the conversations, which, more than talks, are reminiscent of a glorious and buoyant past that will </span>
  <span id="freeText2835345344049339588" style="display:none">Accurate to his elegant style, detailed and generous in the petty small details, Thomas Mann returned with this voluminous novel in which narrates the decline and anguish of a German family of the late nineteenth century.<br />All the complexes that can live (and suffer) people of the upper class, when they see themselves in distressing needs, are described here, with a halo of nostalgia that surrounds the conversations, which, more than talks, are reminiscent of a glorious and buoyant past that will not come back.<br />Tony, daughter of Herr Buddenbrook, says bitterly to his father: "Oh dad, we should never get away from these places, there are a lot of us here. In other places, they would not understand us, and we would suffer because of their gestures, mockery and dishonesty."<br />Tying to the earth (Lübeck), to what it was and isn't anymore, as unconsciously trying to make everything as it was yesterday, that moderates the daily experiences of this family.<br />Like all Thomas Mann's works, the Buddenbrooks is a picture that mixes sociological with psychic, the old with current, the beauty of the past with the harsh reality of the present.<br />A relaxing book, read in one afternoon of copious rain and without great surprises and sincere moments of meditation.</span>

<div id="review_3230170180" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3230170180" />
    <a title="Raul Bimenyimana" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/43664238-raul-bimenyimana"><img alt="Raul Bimenyimana" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1466510542p2/43664238.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3230170180?book_show_action=true">May 04, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Raul Bimenyimana" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Raul Bimenyimana" href="/user/show/43664238-raul-bimenyimana">Raul Bimenyimana</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3230170180" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16423129754233596421">Set in nineteenth century Northern Germany, this is a family saga. The Buddenbrooks are a merchant family recognised in their town for their wealth and status and so each and every personal and professional decision they make is supposed to be an additional honour to the family name.<br/><br/>Spanning four generations, Mann writes of the upper class family's relations with the town and their business, personal lives and marriages. The lives of the third and fourth generational characters being the bulk of</span>
  <span id="freeText16423129754233596421" style="display:none">Set in nineteenth century Northern Germany, this is a family saga. The Buddenbrooks are a merchant family recognised in their town for their wealth and status and so each and every personal and professional decision they make is supposed to be an additional honour to the family name.<br /><br />Spanning four generations, Mann writes of the upper class family's relations with the town and their business, personal lives and marriages. The lives of the third and fourth generational characters being the bulk of the book. This book was 731 pages, it dragged on at certain parts as long books usually do, but the fleshing out of characters left the lasting effect of attachment to them. Done brilliantly, I cringed at their embarrassments, felt proud of their achievements and felt sadness at their losses and grief. I was properly invested in them.<br /><br />This book was written by Thomas Mann when he was just twenty five. It's an impressive long book, written beautifully and quite intense at parts. Like a lot of other debut novels by young writers, this novel drew heavily from the writer’s personal life. Certain observations on life and death and loss were very moving. The bits about music were enthralling, marvellously captured and made me wish I knew more about piano, and violin, and music in general so that I'd have properly appreciated the moments. The tone of the book is nostalgic which can be attributed to Mann reflecting from his past, and that final tragedy which had been long foreshadowed but hurt nonetheless with the end of the book. A wonderful book.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="16423129754233596421" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_610093478" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/610093478" />
    <a title="Gary  the Bookworm" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7036337-gary-the-bookworm"><img alt="Gary  the Bookworm" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1491094498p2/7036337.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/610093478?book_show_action=true">May 08, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Gary  the Bookworm" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Gary  the Bookworm" href="/user/show/7036337-gary-the-bookworm">Gary  the Bookworm</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer610093478" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4286609212571209783">I have been stalling about writing a review. Just as I stalled about finishing the book. <i>Buddenbrooks</i> didn't exactly grab me in the beginning. The first few chapters seemed to be about a bunch of smug, self-indulgent people who wore elaborate outfits and stuffed themselves without restraint. I barely noticed the two sons, Tom and Christian, and Tony, the golden-haired daughter. If it hadn't been for the discussion threads provided by other members of our group, I may have abandoned it entirely. </span>
  <span id="freeText4286609212571209783" style="display:none">I have been stalling about writing a review. Just as I stalled about finishing the book. <i>Buddenbrooks</i> didn't exactly grab me in the beginning. The first few chapters seemed to be about a bunch of smug, self-indulgent people who wore elaborate outfits and stuffed themselves without restraint. I barely noticed the two sons, Tom and Christian, and Tony, the golden-haired daughter. If it hadn't been for the discussion threads provided by other members of our group, I may have abandoned it entirely. It didn't make much sense to me until one of the threads pointed to the influence of Richard Wagner's operas on Thomas Mann. I've never been a huge Wagner fan, but I've heard people speak about <i>Wagner time.</i> Apparently, it explains how audiences are able to sit for hours once they're under the spell of his music. <br><br>So maybe the elaborate housewarming was something like the opening of <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Der_Ring_des_Nibelungen" rel="nofollow noopener">the Ring Cycle </a>when the gods move into their new castle in Valhalla. The Buddenbrooks, gods of commerce, were announcing to the town that they were rich enough to merit everyone's attention-and envy. Old Mr. Buddenbrook made his money as a war profiteer and had recently disinherited an elder son from an earlier marriage, because that son had married a woman from a lower social rank. Was the estranged son, Gotthold, analogous to the giants who built Wotan's castle? They all felt similarly cheated. Despite these vexing thoughts, gods will be gods, so nothing could stop any of them from celebrating their elaborate new digs. The Buddenbrooks' star, like Wotan's ring of stolen gold, would shine brightly as long as someone in the family could mind the family coffers. Easier said than done.<br><a href="http://s1331.photobucket.com/albums/w581/Garmct/?action=view&amp;current=null_zps9cb92de6.jpg" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380420799i/835197.jpg" alt="Photobucket Pictures, Images and Photos" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a><br>The children grew into adulthood knowing what is expected of them; girls were to marry for money and boys would inherit the business and produce male heirs. As long as there was enough wealth, a few youthful indiscretions and missteps shouldn't matter. Unfortunately, except for Grandpa, none of the men had much talent for business and the girls made unfortunate marriages. Things rapidly deteriorated. By the time Tom's son, Hanno, was born, the business was hemorrhaging money, Tony was a divorced, social-outcast and Christian was a disreputable hypochondriac. Mann chose to describe all of this in a series of operatic set pieces. We witness weddings, funerals, intimate gatherings and elaborate family spectacles with all the Buddenbrooks performing as if they were actors on a stage. Each of them has at least one stirring aria, to explain his or her interior life, but they all seem to lack the will-or capacity- to reverse the family's downward trajectory.<br><br>Their plight reminded me of a psychoanalytic theory espoused by Alice Miller, in <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alice_Miller_(psychologist)" rel="nofollow noopener">The Drama of the Gifted Child</a>. There she described a child who was so aware, consciously or otherwise, of the wishes of his parents and had such a strong desire to fulfill them, that he loses track of himself and his own identity. It’s about the child who never discovered his “true self” because he was so concerned with pleasing those around him, and the repercussions of that later in life. The book discusses the unconscious wishes of the parent being often unconsciously bestowed on the child, with the child absorbing these wishes and morphing into this different person. Alice Miller must have read <i>Buddenbrooks</i>!<br><br>Poor Hanno didn't stand a chance. His short life was so mired in unhappiness that it is almost unbearable to read about it. His father's deepening depression, the family's suffocating expectations, unremitting social humiliations and a sadistic dentist caused him to retreat into a world of improvisational music. I kept wishing that his Aunt Tony would morph into Brunhilde <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[<a href="http://s1331.photobucket.com/albums/w581/Garmct/?action=view&amp;current=null_zps1db8a801.jpg" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener"><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380420799i/835198.jpg" alt="Photobucket Pictures, Images and Photos" class="gr-hostedUserImg"></a>  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> and carry him away to Valhalla, like she did for Sieglinde in <i>Die Walkure.</i> Of course that didn't work out too well for Brunhilde and, besides, poor Tony's narcissism kept her from ever anticipating just how bad things might get.<br><br>It was almost a relief to read about Hanno's "immolation" from typhus, which reminded me of the burning of Valhalla in the Ring's final segment, <i>Twilight of the Gods</i>. In Wagner's version the stolen gold is returned to the Rhine-maidens, but for the Buddenbrooks there was nothing left to return. Their happiness, and the gold it was traded for, was long gone. Mann's genius rests in his ability to make us care about these fatally-flawed characters who, because they lacked their own fully-formed identities, were tragic prisoners of their unfortunate childhoods. How ironic.</["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></span>
  <a data-text-id="4286609212571209783" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2194390975" />
    <a title="W.D. Clarke" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/13122795-w-d-clarke"><img alt="W.D. Clarke" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1535550502p2/13122795.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2194390975?book_show_action=true">Apr 11, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="W.D. Clarke" class="user" itemprop="url" name="W.D. Clarke" href="/user/show/13122795-w-d-clarke">W.D. Clarke</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13122795-w-d-clarke?shelf=2019">2019</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2194390975" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18132137189975742237">I am normally not all that into the Grand Family Saga, but this was eminently, compulsively readable, something which, alas was part of its problem for me, as I usually go in for the kind of books whose inventive use of language challenges me in well nigh every paragraph, and though there are times in this novel when the narrative takes on a thrilling intensity—in my mind combining the baleful bitterness of one of Conrad's narrators (I am thinking of <i>Nostromo</i> here) with the synoptic knowingness </span>
  <span id="freeText18132137189975742237" style="display:none">I am normally not all that into the Grand Family Saga, but this was eminently, compulsively readable, something which, alas was part of its problem for me, as I usually go in for the kind of books whose inventive use of language challenges me in well nigh every paragraph, and though there are times in this novel when the narrative takes on a thrilling intensity—in my mind combining the baleful bitterness of one of Conrad's narrators (I am thinking of <i>Nostromo</i> here) with the synoptic knowingness of George Eliot's <i>Middlemarch</i>—as a whole, though, this debut novel falls somewhat short of those two mature masterpieces, so perhaps I shouldn't compare (though I can't help but do). <br /><br />I really should be at a desk studying Ulysses for my groupread, but currently lack the time for that, and this book is exactly the kind of reclining-in-bed companion I can handle right now, one that still stimulates and rewards in equal measure in just such a context. <br /><br />So: 3.5 enjoyable (if the remorselessness and blindness of fortune, mostly bad over a time span of decades can be said to be enjoyable) stars.</span>
  <a data-text-id="18132137189975742237" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>
